<answer from>



slinkies
Member since: 05 June 2007
Total points: 11999 (Level 6)



Contributing In:



Diet & Fitness



</answer from>



<answer>



you should see a doctor to see how bad it is ...  there is a medication you can take if you have it to a certain severity ...



"The phenomenon is more common in women than men, with the Framingham Study finding that 5.8% of men and 9.6% of women suffered from it.



Raynaud's phenomenon, in medicine, is a vasospastic disorder causing discoloration of the fingers, toes, and occasionally other extremities. This condition can also cause nails to become brittle with longitudinal ridges. Named for French physician Maurice Raynaud (1834 - 1881), the cause of the phenomenon is believed to be the result of vasospasms that decrease blood supply to the respective regions. Emotional stress and cold are classic triggers of the phenomenon, and the discoloration follows a characteristic pattern in time: white, blue and red.



It comprises both Raynaud's disease (primary Raynaud's), where the phenomenon is idiopathic,[1] and Raynaud's syndrome (secondary Raynaud's), where it is caused by some other instigating factor. Measurement of hand-temperature gradients is one tool used to distinguish between the primary and secondary forms.[2]



It is possible for the primary form to progress to the secondary form."



Source(s):



wiki ...
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Raynaud_phe...



</answer>



<answer from>



foschizl...
Member since: 31 October 2007
Total points: 1073 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



it's spelled RAYNAUD'S...



Raynaud's disease is a condition that causes some areas of your body — such as your fingers, toes, tip of your nose and your ears — to feel numb and cool in response to cold temperatures or stress. In Raynaud's disease, arteries that supply blood to your skin narrow, limiting blood circulation to affected areas.



Women are more likely to have Raynaud's disease. It's also more common in people who live in colder climates.



Treatment of Raynaud's disease depends on its severity and the presence of associated conditions. For most people, Raynaud's disease is more a nuisance than a disability.



Signs and symptoms include:



Sequence of color changes in your skin in response to cold or stress



Numb, prickly feeling or stinging pain upon warming or relief of stress



During an attack of Raynaud's, affected areas of your skin usually turn white at first. Then, the areas often turn blue and feel cold and numb, and your sensory perception is dulled. As circulation improves, the affected areas may turn red, throb, tingle or swell. The order of the changes of color isn't the same for all people, and not everyone experiences all three colors.



Occasionally, an attack affects just one or two fingers or toes. Attacks don't necessarily always affect the same digits. Although Raynaud's most commonly affects your fingers and toes, the condition can also affect other areas of your body, such as your nose, lips, ears and even nipples. An attack may last less than a minute to several hours.



People who have Raynaud's accompanied by another disease will likely also have signs and symptoms related to their underlying condition.



Doctors don't completely understand the cause of Raynaud's attacks, but blood vessels in the hands and feet appear to overreact to cold temperatures or stress:



Cold temperatures. When your body is exposed to cold temperatures, your extremities lose heat. Your body slows down blood supply to your fingers and toes to preserve your body's core temperature. Your body specifically reduces blood flow by narrowing the small arteries under the skin of your extremities. In people with Raynaud's, this normal response is exaggerated.



Stress. Stress causes a similar reaction to cold in the body, and likewise the body's response may be exaggerated in people with Raynaud's.



Blood vessels in spasm. With Raynaud's, arteries to your fingers and toes go into what's called vasospasm. This constricts the vessels dramatically and temporarily limits blood supply. Over time, these same small arteries may also thicken slightly, further limiting blood flow. The result is that affected skin turns a pale and dusky color due to the lack of blood flow to the area. Once the spasms subside and blood returns to the area, the tissue may turn red before returning to a normal color.



Cold temperatures are most likely to provoke an attack. Exposure to cold can be as simple as putting your hands under a faucet of running cold water, taking something out of the freezer or exposure to cold air. For some people, exposure to cold temperatures isn't necessary. Emotional stress alone can cause an episode of Raynaud's.



Some researchers are studying whether Raynaud's may be partly an inherited disorder.



Primary vs. secondary Raynaud's



Raynaud's occurs in two main types:



Primary Raynaud's. This is Raynaud's without an underlying disease or associated medical problem that could provoke vasospasm. Also called Raynaud's disease, it's the most common form of the disorder.



Secondary Raynaud's. Also called Raynaud's phenomenon, this form is caused by an underlying problem. Although secondary Raynaud's is less common than the primary form, it tends to be a more serious disorder. Signs and symptoms of secondary Raynaud's usually first appear at later ages — around 40 — than they do for people with the primary form of Raynaud's.



Causes of secondary Raynaud's include:



Scleroderma
Lupus



Rheumatoid arthritis



Sjogren's syndrome



Diseases of the arteries



Carpal tunnel syndrome



Repetitive trauma



Smoking



Prior Injuries to the hands or feet



Certain medications
Chemical exposure
Thyroid gland disorders



Source(s):



http://www.mayoclinic.com/health/raynaud...



</answer>

<answer from>



saved_by...
Member since: 21 September 2006
Total points: 30757 (Level 7)



</answer from>



<answer>



Leg cramps are often caused by a shortage in potassium. If the strawberries are working then a banana will work even better. A serving of strawberries has 233 mg of potassium. One banana has 422 mg of potassium.



A strawberry is a superstar when it comes to anti-oxidant power, says Dr. Barry Sears in his book The Top 100 Zone Foods. In addition, 1 cup of strawberries gives you a whopping 140 percent of your recommended daily allowance of vitamin C. Strawberries are also packed with flavonoids, two in particular, called quercetin and kaempferol. Research shows that these two flavonoids help keep “bad” (LDL) cholesterol from oxidizing and damaging artery walls.



Strawberries also contain ellargic acid — also found in raspberries, blackberries, cranberries, grapes, cherries, walnuts, pecans and Brazil nuts — which acts as a scavenger to “bind” cancer-causing chemicals, making them inactive. It inhibits the ability of other chemicals to cause mutations in bacteria. In addition, it prevents binding of carcinogens to DNA and reduces the incidence of cancer in cultured human cells exposed to carcinogens.



</answer>

<question from>



dlcm1979
Member since: 06 April 2007
Total points: 6174 (Level 5)



</question from>



<question>



What could have caused my Dad's death?



My step Dad was diagnosed with Lung Cancer on March 11th 2008. He was given chemo and radiation and went into remission. Every check showed him to be in radical remission. Sept 11, he had a massive heart attack, he had 2 stents put in and was doing really well. After a month, he went to have a full check up and was given a clean bill of health. The next day he felt fine all day, then started coughing up large amounts of blood. He died. He couldn't breath. Any suggestions on what could have caused this? No autopsy was done. My mom couldn't stand the thought of that. It is killing me wondering what happend. I believe in God..and that it was his time...I just wonder what happend...wouldn't you?



</question>



<answer from>



odzooker...
Member since: 18 September 2008
Total points: 1781 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



He may not have had any obvious cancerous growths, which to a doctor spells "remission." since they cannot find any gross or "proximate" cause for cancer, and at the time showed no active malignant growths.
But here you may want to borrow an anatomy text to find out the structures of the lungs and how they work, plus the organs that border and depend on. The one thing that will stand out is the fragility of lung tissue--it must be very thin so that cells bringing oxygen can travel through their walls one way  while cells bearing CO2 travel the other way. The alveoli, or air sacs, are thinner than tissue paper but must be full of blood cells. Furthermore, the lungs are fed oxygen from the pulmonary arteries, which generate substantial , both from the heart and from their own surrounding  muscle tissue.



So when the lungs are thinned out, the body's own natural processes can overwhelm their tissues, and blood starts leaking. Naturally, the body orders coughing to relieve the organs of unwanted water, and sooner or later parts of the lungs just give way.



I don't mean to belittle the terrible ordeal he and his loved ones went through, but sometimes knowledge can help--and with this info you can go to your pulmonary and cardiac specialists with "how" and use it as a door to "why."



</answer>



<asker's comment>



Your right about knowledge helping. I am not looking for someone to blame. I just wonder what happend. I appreciate your thorough answer. Very much. Thank you for taking the time to explain this to me.



</asker's comment>



<answer from>



Steven S
Member since: 28 June 2006
Total points: 323 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>

I'm sorry this happened. I watched my grandfather do basically the same thing when I was little. Cancer is a terrible disease, I wish it would go away.....



</answer>

<answer>



I'm not going to get preachy on you. Just give you the facts. At that age your emotional and physical states are still developing. Using any kind of drug or alcohol effects the development of your physical and psychological well being. Also your hormones are already out of wack at that age, so adding any mind altering chemicals can alter your actual perception and the perceptions of doctors as to whether or not you are developing properly. It can also lead to misdiagnosis of serious medical issues. And of course it is the "gateway drug" so starting at a young age will most like lead quickly to other drugs and could seriously effect your quality of life as you get older.Best bet: stay away until you are older if at all.

Past Experience and Medical Knowledge

<answer from>



Glombool
Member since: 07 September 2008
Total points: 150 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



At 13, a person has not fully developed the awareness of consequence (not an opinion, that's a psychology study).  You can talk all about the effects of narcotics on the body, but it doesn't seem to resonate.



When we are that young, we have a tendency to do stupid things just because someone suggests it... or we think it sounds cool.  We don't think "hmm, how will this affect me a few years down the road.  How will it affect others if it kills me or makes me a quadriplegic, or fries my brain so I have no sense of reality."



By asking the question, you obviously know there is something wrong with it,  because you wouldn't be asking if you didn't care.  The problem is consequence.  It is a "gateway" drug.  It is addicting.  Some people say it isn't, but ask anyone who is addicted to it, or has recovered from the addiction and they will tell you otherwise.  "Social" drug use leads to more social drug use, which leads to addiction.



Oh, and for all you know, the joints could be laced with Meth.  There have been a lot of cases of that around here.  High School kids drug tested who readily admitted to smoking weed, but had not used meth, tested positive for meth.  The joints were laced without their knowledge.  Great way for the meth sellers to get people addicted.



If you have the "not me" mentality... stop it.  Yes, you.  You can become addicted.



If you have the "I can stop any time I want, I just don't want to" mentality... stop it.  You're already addicted. Own up to it.  You don't want to stop because you're addicted, duh.



If you tried it once and that was it... ok, fine.  We all do stupid things.  We try things we shouldn't even though people from experience will tell us how stupid it is.  Don't do it again.  Learn from other people's mistakes as much as you can instead of learning the hard way.



</answer>

cнєlѕєα …εïз
Member since: 02 October 2008
Total points: 131 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



Of course it will lead to infection. However, if you change the staple to a regular surgical metal earring, (sterilized), then you will be fine.



If you do not treat your ear (the cheapest way is to use ear buds with methylated spirits or try a sugar-free listerine), you can end up with swelling, pain and infection.



You can attempt taking the staple out now, but you are told by professional piercer to wait 6 weeks.



Use a sleeper to begin with. Studs collect bacteria around the back.



Good luck and don't be so silly next time. Use a sterilized safety pin! You can quickly sterile with alcohol or an open flame.
Never keep the staple/pin in your ear lobe, change it right away.



Source(s):



I have many ear piercings and my friend re-pierces her ears/face often with safety pins! Cheap and effective!



</answer>

<answer from>



vertigo_...
Member since: 05 August 2006
Total points: 505 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



I don't know if that's a question or a suggestion, but being a dog lover (and a doctor), i think that is a wrong notion.



Whenever any1 is having a fit, human or animal, nothing should be fed to them. They can aspirate it into their lungs and die of suffocation. Fits will not kill themselves.



Try to insert a small folded piece of cloth between the teeth so that it does not bite its tongue. Take the dog to the vet, who will administer injection of anti-seizure medication.



But the golden rule is not to feed anything and turn the dog to its sides so that saliva is drained out. Make sure its mouth is clean, take out any food or bone that may be inside the mouth, this can suffocate and kill the animal.



Source(s):



I am a doctor (for humans!)



</answer>

<question from>



fxcklove
Member since: 29 June 2008
Total points: 90 (Level 1)



</question from>



<question>



Can i put in a new belly button piercing while the hole is still healing.?



i got my belly button piercing like 2 weeks ago, and the bar for the ring is too short and so the ball on the top is going into my skin, and i wanna change the ring so the bar is alittle bit longer. but i heard that it will instantly close or it'll get infected if i put in a new ring while the hole is still healing. but i don't know if it's true or not. help me please?



</question>



<answer from>



Matthew S
Member since: 21 October 2008
Total points: 157 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



If the bar is too short, it's probably from the bit of swelling that a new piercing gets--you're piercer probably misjudged. This, too, won't help it heal-up. So yes, you should get this changed-out for a different piece, but NO you should not do it yourself.



It won't instantly close if you take it out but give it a short amount of time and it'll be very difficult (not to mention painful) to get another piece of jewelry in there.



Take it to a piercer, even another piercer, and have them give you a bigger barbell (or bananabell as the curved ones are called more often).



The problem with doing it yourself is definitely infection but even with the cleanest hands and if you cleaned the area first, AND cleaned the new jewelry, it might still get an infection because of the type of metal you'd be putting in it. Some metals "approved" as body jewelry are really only safe for piercings that are healed, as opposed to other types of metal jewelry that are more hypo-allergenic in general.



Source(s):



I've had 8 body mods including tongue, labret, and a bunch of eyebrow rings.



</answer>



<asker's comment>



thank you so much :)



</asker's comment>

<question>



I have been taking 100 mg's of Zoloft?



For about 7 months now.



I was just wondering how long it would take it to get out of my system
if i just completely stopped taking it.



</question>



<answer from>



yessaipa...
Member since: 19 March 2008
Total points: 2625 (Level 4)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



2-3 weeks depending on your body's metabolism.  It is possible to be clear of it with 72 hours with a proper flush (dangerous if done without professional).



That being said, Zoloft is one of those drugs you want to be weaned off slowly.  To rapidly stop taking it can cause many unpleasant effects which can include deep depression, suicide, seizures, heart problems, and death.  If you want to get off it, do so with the help of a professional.  They should know your history and medical condition and be able to help you do it in the shortest amount of time.



</answer>



<asker's comment>



Amazing,
Thank you!
And i weaned off from 100mgs to 50 mgs to nothing.
Im feeling fine now!
Just wanted to know how long it would take to be out of my system! : )



</asker's comment>



<answer from>



Ivy
Member since: 28 April 2008
Total points: 724 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



You should never just stop taking any medicine cold turkey.  Your body is used to having it every day you should wean yourself off slowly and under a doctors care.  There are certain antidepressant's that if you stop cold turkey you are at risk for a seizure or just feeling nauseous or getting headaches. I am not saying that zoloft is necessarily one of them or that this will happen but don't take the chance.



As you wean down less will stay in your system and when you finally do get off of it completely it will be out of your system within a couple of weeks.



Source(s):



Experience with Antidepressant's



</answer>



<answer from>



parkplac...
Member since: 17 July 2008
Total points: 657 (Level 2)

http://www.findingdulcinea.com/guides.to...

Light smoker or acute dosage 1-3 days

Oral ingestion 1-5 days
